      <div class="container first-row" data-reactid=".i624dl6mf4.5.1:2.1">
       <div class="article-row row-md" data-reactid=".i624dl6mf4.5.1:2.1.0">
        <div class="article-main col-md-8 col-xl-9 col-auto article-content-body" data-reactid=".i624dl6mf4.5.1:2.1.0.0">
         <figure class="main-image landscape" data-reactid=".i624dl6mf4.5.1:2.1.0.0.0" data-rendition="xlarge-900x600">
          <img alt="" data-reactid=".i624dl6mf4.5.1:2.1.0.0.0.0" height="434" src="http://photos.vanityfair.com/2015/01/29/54caaa87494254fc099683b0_image.png" width="640"/>
          <figcaption class="caption main-image-caption" data-reactid=".i624dl6mf4.5.1:2.1.0.0.0.1" style="max-width:640px;">
           <div class="title" data-reactid=".i624dl6mf4.5.1:2.1.0.0.0.1.0">
            The costumes of Django Unchained.
           </div>
           <span data-reactid=".i624dl6mf4.5.1:2.1.0.0.0.1.1">
           </span>
          </figcaption>
         </figure>
         <span data-reactid=".i624dl6mf4.5.1:2.1.0.0.2">
         </span>
         <div class="byline" data-reactid=".i624dl6mf4.5.1:2.1.0.0.3">
          <div class="contributors" data-reactid=".i624dl6mf4.5.1:2.1.0.0.3.$Author">
           <label data-reactid=".i624dl6mf4.5.1:2.1.0.0.3.$Author.0">
            by
           </label>
           <ul class="list" data-reactid=".i624dl6mf4.5.1:2.1.0.0.3.$Author.1">
            <li class="contributor" data-reactid=".i624dl6mf4.5.1:2.1.0.0.3.$Author.1.$5429a0dd0b6619b370eb9035">
             <a class="author" data-reactid=".i624dl6mf4.5.1:2.1.0.0.3.$Author.1.$5429a0dd0b6619b370eb9035.0" href="/contributor/marnie-hanel" rel="author">
              Marnie Hanel
             </a>
            </li>
           </ul>
          </div>
         </div>
         <span class="content" data-reactid=".i624dl6mf4.5.1:2.1.0.0.5">
          <p>
           <a href="#slideshow">
            <strong>
             Click to see more costume designs from
             <em>
              Django Unchained
             </em>
             .
            </strong>
           </a>
          </p>
          <p>
           <em>
            In a recurring series,
           </em>
           Vanity Fair
           <em>
            pulls back the curtain on awards seasons most visually enticing films, revealing exclusive details of the creative process of art directors, costume designers, makeup artists, cinematographers, and more. This week, two-time Oscar nominee and costume designer Sharen Davis discusses her designs for Quentin Tarantinos
           </em>
           Django Unchained.
          </p>
          <p>
           The Academy Awards nominees wont be announced for another week, but according to an accidentally published test page on Oscars.org,
           <em>
            Django Unchained
           </em>
           costume designer Sharen Davis has already clinched a slot. This is the third Academy Award nomination for Sharen Davis. She was previously nominated for
           <em>
            Dreamgirls
           </em>
           (2006) and
           <em>
            Ray
           </em>
           (2004)
           <a href="http://oscar.go.com/nominees/costume-design/django-unchained">
            the site said.
           </a>
           While we feel sorry for the poor sap responsible for her inclusionand while we wont know whether shes
           <em>
            really
           </em>
           nominated until next weekwe had to tell Davis.
          </p>
          <p>
           Oh my gosh! I havent even lost weight! Davis replied. I never thought about an Oscar in my life. When they called me for
           <em>
            Ray
           </em>
           , I thought it was a prank call. I said, Am I going to open the door and see one of those fake, standing Oscars?
          </p>
          <p>
           Davis was similarly incredulous when the Tarantino camp approached her for
           <em>
            Django Unchained.
           </em>
           Are you sure you want the girl from
           <em>
            The Help
           </em>
           and
           <em>
            Dreamgirls
           </em>
           ? she asked. Although the bold, bloody Western is a departure for DavisI dont even like violent movies, she admitsher admiration for Quentin Tarantino trumped her sensitivity.
          </p>
          <p>
           Early in the process, Davis says she realized two thingsfirst, its a period movie within a period movie. Its set before the Civil War, but its really a spaghetti Western, which is a late-60s, early-70s movie. (In other words, any discussion of whether or not a period detail rings of preCivil War authenticity is kind of irrelevant.)
          </p>
          <div class="embed image landscape row ">
           <figure class="embedded-image">
            <img alt="" border="0" src="http://photos.vanityfair.com/2015/01/29/54caaa86a298661966ee268e_image.jpg" title=""/>
            <figcaption classname="caption callout-image-caption">
             <div class="wrapper">
              <p>
               A screenshot of Oscars.orgs early nomination of Sharen Davis, costume designer of
               <em>
                Django Unchained.
               </em>
               (Click to enlarge).
              </p>
             </div>
            </figcaption>
           </figure>
          </div>
          <p>
           Second, Tarantino had a very specific vision. Its all in the script. Quentin writes everything down. Everything. Django wears a green jacket. I illustrated it as it was written, she says.
          </p>
          <p>
           When, early in the process, the director invited Davis to his house to watch
           <em>
            Bonanza
           </em>
           , she says she came away thinking, I want to make it a rock n roll, spaghetti Western. That meant visiting the hat-makers who made Michael Langdons
           <em>
            Bonanza
           </em>
           topper and finding a variation for Jamie Foxx. Djangos hat is Little Joes, Davis says. I just put a little more swerve in it.
          </p>
          <p>
           It also meant digging up spaghetti-Western eyewear. Sunglasses are usually considered props, but I was asked to find them. I started searching through films that I thought Quentin would like, because thats what he relates to, Davis says. You can show him the actual history, but that wont do it. They were Charles Bronsons from
           <em>
            The White Buffalo
           </em>
           . Theyre hammered metal from 1830. Theyre hard to put on. They just splay! I only had one pair.
          </p>
          <p>
           While Foxx pulls off the sunglasses so effortlessly that we might even see copycat lenses on the street, theres one costume in the film no one could carry. When Dr. King Schultz, a bounty hunter, takes the freed slave Django, to choose a costume for the pairs next con, Django selects a ruffled, ridiculous Little Lord Fauntleroy suit.
          </p>
          <p>
           Quentin had it in the script as powder blue. And I said, I just cant do that. It is very 70s, but thats going to look like polyester no matter what I make it out of. I slipped a copy of Thomas Gainsboroughs
           <em>
            The Blue Boy
           </em>
           in the back of the research book. He didnt say anything, but he saw it. He sort of said later, Oh! Make him look like Blue Boy.
          </p>
          <p>
           Even with the directors go ahead, Davis held her breath for Foxxs approval. I thought, Hes never going to wear this. But he loved it. It was the hardest one to put on, because of the lace-up arms. It took forever. Then I had to build in comfort. I had a different pair of pants for every action. When he was running, Id drop down the crotch a bit and give him a little more in the waist. When he was riding, we didnt want the pants to ride high.
          </p>
          <p>
           Tarantinos gory action sequences presented another challenge. There was so much blood! The squibs kept getting bigger and bigger and bigger, to the point where if they had been still, youd have thought they all had goiters, she says. I would take the clothes off the backs of the actors when they got shot, literally hand wash it, blow dry it, put some kind of paneling in, and they would re-squib it. It was old school.
          </p>
          <p>
           At times, color serves to pace the plot. It doesnt take the viewer long to realize that the whiter a surface, the more likely it is to be splattered: when plantation owner Calvin Candie (Leonardo DiCaprio) arrives at dinner with a white carnation in his buttonhole, he may as well have a bulls-eye on his chest.
          </p>
          <p>
           Quentin said, Id like Calvin to wear a white carnation. And I said, Im just going to ask you now. Is that where hes going to get shot? Because, if it is, Ill get special effects. Quentin wont tell you much in advance, so youre excited to see what happens.
          </p>
          <p>
           To dress Djangos love interest Broomhilda (Kerry Washington), Davis referenced the spaghetti-Western tradition of super-saturated flashbacks and visions. Broomhilda often appears in deep purple, bright yellow, and eye-catching blue. For Broomhildas final, ride-off-into-the-moonlight look, Davis chose a belted full skirt and a button-down shirt. That was her segue into becoming a Western woman. Shes saying, Im as strong as you, honey. Lets go! I illustrated her whole arc. She started in a dark purple and ended in a light purple. Now shes totally free.
          </p>
          <p>
           Clothes emphasize Foxxs arc, too. In his final action sequence, Django stages a surprise attack while wearing Candies suit. Thats what made it the spaghetti Western it should be. Those two could never wear the same clothes! I remember Quentin called me three days before the shoot. He said, I have a great idea. Jamie should wear Leonardos red suit. And I thought, Do I even have enough fabric?
          </p>
          <p>
           The costume designers only disappointment, she says, is that so many costumes never made it to the screen. That leaves some unexplained characters, like that of the beautiful lady outlaw whose face is half-covered throughout the film. (The idea there, says Davis, is that the character would drop the bandana to reveal an absent jaw.) It also leaves the possibility of a sequel.
          </p>
          <p>
           I think
           <em>
            Django 2
           </em>
           is on the cutting-room floor! says the costume designer, lamenting the intricately dressed gangs, comfort slaves, and miscreants who will never be seen. There are barely any clothes in the movie. It breaks my heart!
          </p>
          <p>
           Although, judging by the early response to Daviss work, it seems there are just enough.
          </p>
         </span>
        </div>

        </aside>
